[1] Chapter 1
[2] Prologue
[3] In the grand hall of the Al Rasheed Hotel by the Tigris River in Baghdad, a nurse from a hospital is finishing a letter. Her pen glides smoothly over the paper.
[4] …Well, dear, that's all the news I have for you. I must say, it's a pleasure to see a bit of the world, but please, next time, let's go to England. You can't imagine how dirty and chaotic Baghdad is, not at all like the romantic tales of the Thousand and One Nights! Of course, the view on the river is nice, but the city itself is a mess, with no decent shops. Captain Kirsch took me to the bazaar, and I won't deny that it's quaint, but to me, it's all just useless stuff, and they go on and on about copper trays, which is just annoying. I wouldn't use any of it unless I could be sure I could clean it properly. You have to be careful with the rust on copper dishes.
[5] As for the job Dr. Riley mentioned, I'll write and tell you as soon as I hear anything. He said that the American gentleman is here in Baghdad and may come to visit me this afternoon. It's probably for his wife, who has some "delusions" – that's what Dr. Riley said. He didn't elaborate, of course, but we all know what that usually means. (But I sincerely hope it's not delirium tremens!) Dr. Liddon is an archaeologist who works for an American museum and is currently excavating an ancient tomb in the desert somewhere.
[6] Well, dear, I must stop here. I found your last letter about Mr. Stapleton very amusing! What will the head nurse say?
[7] I won't write more.
[8] Yours always,
[9] Amy Lethern
[10] She addressed the envelope: "Miss Cora Croft, St. Christopher's Hospital, London."
[11] As she capped her pen, a local waiter approached her.
[12] "A gentleman wants to see you. He says he's Dr. Laidner."
[13] Nurse Lethlean turned around to see a medium-sized man with slightly sloping shoulders, brown beard, and gentle but tired eyes.
[14] Dr. Laidner saw a woman of about thirty-five, tall and confident. He saw a cheerful face with slightly protruding blue eyes and lustrous brown hair. He thought she looked just like a nurse who should take care of anxious patients: open, strong, alert, and unassuming.
[15] He thought Nurse Lethlean was just the right person for the job.
[16] Chapter Two
[17] Introducing Amy Lesterland
[18] I don't want to pretend to be a writer or act like I'm good at writing. I'm doing this at the request of Dr. Liley, and for some reason, when Dr. Liley asks you to do something, you don't want to refuse.
[19] "Oh, but doctor," I said, "I have no idea how to write."
[20] "Nonsense!" he said. "You can write it up as a case record if you want."
[21] So, of course, you can see it that way.
[22] Dr. Rely continued. He said that what we urgently needed was an unadorned account of the events at the Jeremiah site.
[23] "If it were written by one of the parties involved, it might be hard to believe. Others would probably think it was biased."
[24] Of course, that was the truth. I could say that I was present throughout the incident, but at the same time, I was a genuine outsider.
[25] "Why don't you write it yourself, Doctor?" I asked.
[26] "I wasn't there, and you were. Besides," he sighed, adding, "my daughter wouldn't let me."
[27] It was a bit embarrassing how much he coddled his little girl. I was about to say something, but then I saw his eyes flicker. That's what makes Dr. Lyle so annoying - you never know if he's joking. He always talks about things in such a slow, melancholy way, but most of the time you can see him winking.
[28] "Okay," I said uncertainly, "I think I can."
[29] "You can, of course."
[30] "I just have no idea where to start."
[31] "That's all part of the process. Start from the beginning and write until you reach the end."
[32] "I don't even know where to begin with this," I hesitated.
[33] "Trust me, Miss Nurse, starting is the easy part. At least for me when I give speeches. They even had to pull at my coat tail to get me off the podium."
[34] "Oh, you're joking, Dr. Leroy."
[35] "I'm quite serious. So, what do you think?"
[36] There's another thing bothering me. After a moment's hesitation, I said, "Doctor, you know, I sometimes feel like maybe I reveal a bit too much... well, personal emotions, you know?"
[37] "Good heavens, madam, the more personal emotions you reveal, the better. You're writing about real people, not dolls! You can express your feelings, have biases, be sarcastic. Do whatever you want! Just write in your own way. We'll just remove any parts that could be considered defamatory later. You're a sensible woman, you'll be able to tell the story in a reasonable way."
[38] So, things were settled, and I agreed to do my best.
[39] Now I'm ready to start writing, but, like I told the doctor, it's hard to know where to begin.
[40] I think I should start by introducing myself. My name is Amy Lethlean, and I'm thirty-two years old. I first trained as a nurse at St. Christopher's Hospital and then worked in obstetrics for two years before doing some private nursing and spending four years at Biddix Ladies' Sanatorium in Devonshire. I accompanied Mrs. Kelsey to Iraq. Previously, I had taken care of her when her daughter was born. She and her husband were going to Baghdad, where they had arranged for a nanny to look after the child. Mrs. Kelsey had friends in Baghdad, and the nanny had been working for them for many years. Since her friend's child was going back to school, the nanny agreed to come work for Mrs. Kelsey after the children left. Mrs. Kelsey was delicate and worried about traveling with such a young child, so Colonel Kelsey arranged for me to accompany his wife and child. They would cover my travel expenses back to England, unless I could find another nursing position on the way back.
[41] I don't see the need to describe the Kirsch family in detail. The baby was small and adorable, and Mrs. Kirsch was good-natured, albeit somewhat anxious. I enjoyed the trip, as it was my first long sea voyage.
[42] Dr. Reilly was also on board the ship. He was a tall, dark-haired man with a long face who liked to tell tales in a low, melancholy voice. I thought he liked to joke with me, telling me the most unusual things and seeing if I would believe them. He was a doctor in a place called Hasani, where he treated the local people. It was still a day and a half's journey from Baghdad.
[43] When I met him again, I had been in Baghdad for about a week. He asked me when I planned to leave the Kirsch family. I said his question was timely, as the Wrights (the friends I mentioned earlier) were planning to return home early, and their nanny would soon be able to come over.
[44] He said he had heard about the Wrights, which was why he asked me.
[45] "Actually, Nurse Miss, I have prepared a job for you."
[46] "Caring for a patient?"
[47] He frowned, as if considering how to phrase it.
[48] "It's difficult to call her a patient. She's actually just a lady who has... well, let's say, some... delusions?"
[49] "Ah!" I said.
[50] (Usually, I know what that means - either alcoholism or drug abuse!)
[51] Dr. Riley didn't elaborate further, and it was clear he was being cautious about the matter. "Yes," he said, "it's Mrs. Liddon. Her husband is an American - or more precisely, an American of Swedish descent - leading a large American archaeological excavation team."
[52] He then went on to explain how the archaeological team was excavating a large Assyrian city site like Nineveh, and their camp was not too far from Hassani, but in a rather remote and desolate area. Dr. Liddon had been concerned about his wife's health for some time.
[53] "His description of his wife's health situation wasn't very clear, but it seems her problem is recurrent anxiety and panic attacks."
[54] "Is she left alone all day at the camp, with only the locals around?"
[55] "Ah, no, there are quite a few people with her, about seven or eight. I don't think she would be left alone at the camp. However, it's clear she's becoming increasingly odd. Dr. Liddon's work is quite demanding, but he's also very attached to his wife, so seeing her in this state worries him greatly. He would feel much more at ease if there was someone with the necessary expertise and responsibility to help take care of her."
[56] "What does Mrs. Liddon think about it?"
[57] Dr. Riley replied gravely, "Mrs. Liddon is a nice woman, but her opinions on things change every couple of days. Overall, she likes the attention. She's also a bit eccentric, overly emotional - I'd say she's quite a skilled liar. However, Dr. Liddon seems to truly believe that his wife is genuinely frightened by something."
[58] "What did she tell you, doctor?"
[59] "Oh, she hasn't said anything to me yet. She doesn't really like me for some reason. It was Dr. Liddon who approached me with this idea. So, Miss, what do you think of this job? The archaeological team will be there for another two months, and I think it would be a good idea for you to learn a bit about the country before you go back, and archaeological excavation is quite an interesting thing to do."
[60] I hesitated for a moment, weighing his words in my mind, and then said, "Well, I think I would be willing to give it a try."
[61] "Great," Dr. Riley said, standing up. "Mr. Laidner is in Baghdad now, and I'll tell him to come over and see if we can settle things face-to-face."
[62] Dr. Laidner arrived at the hotel later that afternoon. He appeared to be a middle-aged man with a slightly nervous demeanor, looking hesitant. However, I could still see a shadow of kindness, warmth, and helplessness in him.
[63] He seemed very loyal to his wife but was clueless about what was bothering her.
[64] "You see," he said, puzzled and tugging at his beard, which I later found out was a habit of his, "My wife is in a very tense and anxious state of mind, and I'm really worried about her."
[65] "Is she physically healthy?" I asked.
[66] "Yes, uh, of course, I think so. I don't think there's anything wrong with her physically. But you know, she just tends to...uh, imagine things."
[67] "What does she imagine?" I asked.
[68] But he quickly changed the subject, mumbling softly, "She just gets upset for no reason, and I don't understand what she's afraid of."
[69] "Dr. Linden, you mean she's afraid of something?"
[70] He stammered, "Oh, you know, just a little anxious and fearful."
[71] I thought to myself, it's likely she's addicted to drugs, and he has no idea. Many men don't realize. They just wonder why their wives act so nervous, restless, and have such drastic mood swings.
[72] I asked him if Mrs. Redner agreed to me coming over to take care of her.
[73] He lit up at this.
[74] "She agreed. I'm surprised and happy about it. She said it's a great idea, and it makes her feel safer."
[75] This caught me off guard. Safer—that's a strange way to put it. I began to wonder if Mrs. Redner might indeed be a psychotic patient.
[76] He went on eagerly, like a child.
[77] "I'm sure you'll get along with her just fine. She's a very attractive woman." He smiled at me sincerely, "She thinks you'll be her biggest comfort, and I feel the same way about you. I'm sorry to be presumptuous, but you look very healthy, and you seem well-traveled, so I'm certain you're the perfect choice for Louise."
[78] "Well, Dr. Ladden, I'll give it a try." I said cheerfully, "I sincerely hope I can be of help to your wife. Do you think she might just be a little nervous about being around locals and people of color?"
[79] "Oh, not at all." He chuckled at the idea, shaking his head, "My wife absolutely loves Arabs, especially their simplicity and humor. We've only been married for less than two years, and this is her second archaeological season, but she's already picked up quite a bit of Arabic."
[80] I paused for a moment, then prepared to try again.
[81] "Dr. Laidner, can you tell me what your wife is afraid of?" I asked.
[82] He hesitated for a moment, then slowly said, "I hope – I believe – she'll tell you herself."
[83] These were all the details I could extract from him.
[84] Chapter 3
[85] Gossip
[86] As arranged, I was supposed to depart the following week for the Jarmery site.
[87] Mrs. Kirsch was busy settling into her house in Alvia, and I was glad to lend a hand to ease her burden.
[88] During that time, I also heard some news about the Liddell expedition from others. Mrs. Kirsch's friend was an Air Force major, who exclaimed with surprise, "Lovely Louise! That's what she's been up to lately!" Then he turned to me, "Nurse, that's what we all call her, and she's known by that name."
[89] "Is she very pretty?" I asked.
[90] "That's according to her own standards. She thinks she's beautiful!"
[91] "Don't be too mean, John," said Mrs. Kylhe. "You know very well that it's not just her who thinks so! Many people are crazy about her."
[92] "Maybe you're right. She's a bit older, but she's still quite attractive."
[93] "You fell for her too, didn't you?" Mrs. Kylhe said with a smile.
[94] The Air Force major blushed and sheepishly admitted, "Yes, she has a kind of charm that's hard to resist. For Ledna himself, he would even want to burn incense and worship wherever she has been, and the other members of the archaeological team would have to follow suit, of course!"
[95] "How many people are there in total?" I asked.
[96] "There were people from all over the world, including a British architect, a French priest from Carthage who specialized in deciphering inscriptions on stones and other artifacts, a Miss Johnson from Britain who managed various tasks, a short and stout American in charge of photography, the Mocados, whose origin is unknown, possibly Italy or Spain, and the Mocado's wife, who is very young and seems to be a bit cunning. She also dislikes our charming Louise. There are also two young people, that's all. Some of them are a bit odd, but overall, they are quite good. What do you think, Penneyman?"
[97] He was seeking the opinion of an older man, who was sitting there deep in thought, twirling a pair of spectacles.
[98] He was startled by his words and quickly looked up.
[99] "Yes, yes, they are all quite good. Each one is fine. Of course, Mocado is a bit odd…"
[100] "His beard is strange," Mrs. Kershaw interjected, "it looks soft and limp."
[101] Colonel Penryman did not pay any attention to Mrs. Kelsey's remark and continued to speak.
[102] "The two young people are quite good. The American is quite quiet, and the British boy is quite talkative. It's interesting because it's usually the other way around. Laiden is a likable person, very modest and unassuming. Yes, they are all quite likable people. But for some reason, perhaps it's just my imagination, I always felt something was off when I visited them a few days ago. I don't know what it was…no one seemed very natural. There was a strange tension in the air. Perhaps I can explain it better by saying that they were too polite when passing the butter to each other."
[103] I don't like to speak too much, so I blush a little when I speak. "I think that if people are stuck together for too long, they may become irritable. I have had this experience in the hospital."
[104] "You make sense," said Colonel Kelsey, "but this expedition has just begun, and such feelings should not arise so quickly."
[105] "An archaeological team can be like a microcosm of everyday life," said Colonel Penryman, "with cliques, rivalries, and jealousy. "
[106] "I heard they came with several new people this year," said Captain Kershaw.
[107] "Let me count," said the Air Force squadron leader, "Young Coleman is new, so is Lattel. Emmott came last year, as did the Macado couple. Father Lavigne is new, replacing Dr. Bird, who couldn't come this year due to illness. Keller, of course, is an old face, he's been in the team since the beginning five years ago. And Miss Johnson has been here almost as long as Keller."
[108] "I always felt that everyone got along well at the Yariamia site," commented Captain Kershaw. "They looked like a happy family. But considering human nature, this harmony is the most surprising thing. I'm sure Nurse Lethlan would agree with me."
[109] "Well," I said, "I don't think there's anything wrong with what you said. The conflicts that occur in hospitals are usually over trivial matters."
[110] "Yes, people tend to become petty when they spend too much time in a closed circle," said Captain Pennington. "But I still think there's more to it. Mr. Ladden is a particularly gentle and humble person, and he always knows how to make his team members get along well. But I still felt that tense atmosphere that day."
[111] Mrs. Kelly chuckled.
[112] "Can't you see why? It's obvious, isn't it?"
[113] "What do you mean?"
[114] "Of course, it's Lethan's wife."
[115] "Come on, Mary," said her husband. "She's a charming woman, not the type to argue."
[116] "I didn't say she likes to argue, but she makes others argue."
[117] "How? Why?"
[118] "Why? Why? She gets bored, that's why! She's not an archaeologist, just an archaeologist's wife. Being cut off from the outside world for too long makes her bored, so she decides to put on a show. Stirring up trouble, causing divisions, and then enjoying the spectacle."
[119] "Mary, those are just your imaginings, and you don't know what you're talking about."
[120] "Of course they are, but you'll find I'm right. Charming Louise wouldn't make that Mona Lisa face for no reason. She may not have any malicious intent, but she just wants to see what happens."
[121] "She's deeply in love with Lieden."
[122] "Ah, that's not necessarily true. I'm not saying there's anything underhanded going on, but I can tell you that this woman is definitely a woman of passion [1]."
[123] "You women are really giving each other face," said Colonel Kelsh.
[124] "I understand, you men always say we're mean and petty, but women still understand women better."
[125] "Well, that's true," said Colonel Penniman thoughtfully, "even if all of Colonel Kelsh's slightly snide conjectures are true, I still can't explain the strange tension. It's like a storm is coming. I have a strong feeling that something is about to happen."
[126] "Don't scare our nurse miss," said Mrs. Kersh, "she's going there in three days, and you'll scare her off."
[127] "Ah, you won't scare me," I said with a smile.
[128] Despite this, I pondered over the words I had heard. "Safer," the strange phrase from Dr. Laidner came back to my mind. Could it be that Mrs. Laidner's vague, mysterious fear affected the other members of the archaeological team? Or was it the real tension (or the unknown cause of it) that affected her mental state?
[129] I looked up the term "flirt" in the dictionary, but couldn't quite figure out its meaning.
[130] "Well," I said to myself, "we'll see."

[132] [1] The original French term "allumeuse" refers to a woman who flirts and tries to seduce men.
[133] Chapter 4
[134] Arrival in Hassani
[135] Three days later, I left Baghdad.
[136] Leaving Mrs. Kitchener and her daughter made me a little sad. The little girl was so cute and growing up, gaining weight every week. Colonel Kitchener accompanied me to the station and saw me off. I expected to arrive in Kirkuk the next morning, where someone would pick me up.
[137] I slept poorly; I never sleep well on trains, always dreaming. However, when I looked out the window the next morning, I saw a clear sky, which made me curious and excited to meet the people I would see.
[138] I stood on the platform hesitantly looking around when I saw a young man approaching me. He had a round face and a pinkish complexion. To be honest, I had never seen anyone who looked so much like a character from a P.G. Woodhouse book.
[139] "Hello, hello, hello," he said, "Are you Nurse Latham? I think you are, I can tell. Ha ha, I'm Coleman, Dr. Lethem sent me to pick you up. How are you? The journey must have been tiring, I know the feeling of traveling on this kind of train! Okay, let's go, have you had breakfast? Is this your luggage? I must say, it's quite simple, isn't it? Mrs. Lethem has four suitcases and a large trunk, not to mention a hatbox, a novelty pillow, and a whole lot of other things. Am I talking too much? Come on, let's get into that old car."
[140] A car was parked outside the station, which they later called a traveling car. It looked a bit like a four-wheeled light carriage, a bit like a cargo truck, and a bit like a small car. Mr. Coleman helped me into the car and advised me to sit next to the driver to avoid too much bouncing.
[141] Bouncing! I didn't know if this strange thing would be bounced into pieces! The road was not like a road at all, just a bumpy little path. Is this really the splendid civilization of the East? I couldn't help but miss the smooth roads of England.
[142] Mr. Coleman sat behind me. He leaned forward and shouted into my ear.
[143] "This road is quite good!" he shouted when we were bounced up, almost hitting the car's ceiling.
[144] And apparently, he was sincere.
[145] "It's good for your liver to be active," he said, "You should know that, Nurse Miss."
[146] "I don't think it would do me any good if my head cracked open, and my liver was still excited," I retort sarcastically.
[147] "You should come after the rain," he suggests. "The cars slip around more then, and we're mostly driving sideways."
[148] I have no response to that.
[149] Soon we need to cross the river. We're on the wildest ferry you can imagine. I feel lucky we made it across, but it seems everyone else thinks it's perfectly normal.
[150] It takes us four hours to reach Hachani. To my surprise, it's a rather large place. It looks beautiful from the other side of the river before we cross, with white spires standing like a fairyland. But once you walk across the bridge, it's a different story. Everything looks shaky, dilapidated, and smells unpleasant; mud everywhere, a mess.
[151] Mr. Coleman took me to Dr. Lyle's residence. He said the doctor was waiting for me to have lunch.
[152] Dr. Lyle was as friendly as ever, and his house exuded warmth. There was a bathroom, and everything was spotless. I felt great after a nice shower and putting on my work clothes.
[153] Lunch was ready, and we went into the dining room. Dr. Lyle apologized for his daughter being late. She came in just as we finished a delicious dish of hard-boiled eggs. Dr. Lyle introduced me to his daughter, saying, "This is Miss Smith, the nurse."
[154] She shook my hand, hoping my journey was pleasant, and then took off her hat, giving Mr. Coleman a cold nod, and sat down.
[155] "Hi, Bill," she said. "How are you?"
[156] He started talking to her about an upcoming party at the club, and I took the opportunity to observe her.
[157] I can't say I really liked her. In my opinion, she seemed a bit cold. Although she was good-looking, she came across as impolite. She had black hair and blue eyes, pale skin, and red lipstick. Her sarcastic way of speaking really annoyed me. There was once an intern who followed me who was just like her, although I have to admit that the girl did a great job, but her behavior always made me angry.
[158] It seemed that Mr. Coleman was infatuated with her. He became a bit stuttering and said things that were more ridiculous than before. His behavior reminded me of a dog wagging its tail to please people.
[159] After lunch, Dr. Riley went to the hospital, and Mr. Coleman went to the city to buy some things. Dr. Riley's daughter asked if I wanted to go sightseeing in the city or stay at home. She said that Mr. Coleman would come to pick me up in about an hour.
[160] "Is there anything to see?" I asked.
[161] "There are some interesting places," Miss Leroy said, "but I'm not sure if you'll like them, as they're all very messy."
[162] This way of speaking irritated me because I couldn't understand how one could describe messiness as interesting.
[163] In the end, she took me to the club, which was comfortable enough to overlook the river and had English newspapers and magazines to read.
[164] When we returned to our residence, Mr. Coleman was still not back, so we sat down to talk. For some reason, this was not a relaxed conversation.
[165] She asked me if I had met Mrs. Ladden.
[166] "No," I said, "I've only met her husband."
[167] "Ah," she said, "I was wondering what you thought of her."
[168] I didn't reply to this, and she went on: "I like Dr. Liddon very much, everyone does."
[169] This seemed to mean that she didn't like his wife.
[170] I still didn't speak. After a moment, she suddenly asked me, "What's the matter with her, didn't Dr. Liddon tell you?"
[171] I didn't want to gossip about her before seeing the patient, so I vaguely said: "I just know she's not feeling well and needs care."
[172] She laughed, a very malicious laugh that was both piercing and rude.
[173] "Goodness," she said. "Doesn't she have enough people taking care of her already?"
[174] "I think they all have their own jobs to do," I said.
[175] "Jobs? They certainly do have jobs, but Louise is the most important one. She's the one making sure of that."
[176] "Yeah, I guess you don't like her," I thought to myself.
[177] "Even so," Miss Riley continued, "I still don't understand why she'd want a professional nurse from the hospital. I always thought an amateur helper would be more her style. She doesn't need someone to take her temperature or pulse, and then do everything perfectly."
[178] I had to admit, my curiosity was piqued.
[179] "Do you think she's not even sick?" I asked.
[180] "Of course she's not sick! That woman is as sturdy as a bull. 'Dear Louise hasn't slept yet.' 'She has dark circles under her eyes.' Of course she does, she just smudges them with blue pencil! All she wants is attention, to have everyone revolve around her and make a fuss over her!"
[181] I knew she had a point. I had seen many cases of hypochondria (what nurse hasn't?) who liked to have their entire family fussing over them. If a doctor or nurse told them, "You're perfectly fine," they would first not believe it, and then get very angry, putting on a show of being as upset as possible.
[182] Of course, Mrs. Laidner could very well be one of those patients. In such a case, her husband would naturally be the first to fall for it. I found that when it came to illness, husbands were always very gullible. But even so, I still felt that what I had heard didn't quite add up. For example, she wouldn't use the phrase "safer" in this context.
[183] It was strange how those words kept playing in my mind.
[184] As I thought about this, I asked, "Is Mrs. Laidner a nervous woman? For example, would she feel nervous being out in such a remote place?"
[185] "Good heavens, what's there to be nervous about? They have ten people there! And they have guards, too, to protect the ancient ruins. She has nothing to be nervous about, at least—"
[186] She seemed to be interrupted by some thought, pausing for a moment before continuing.
[187] "That's odd for you to say."
[188] "Why?"
[189] "A few days ago, I went there with Air Force Lieutenant Jervis. Most of them were at the excavation site. She was sitting there writing a letter, and I think she didn't hear us come. The servant who usually shows guests in wasn't there that day, so we walked all the way to the corridor. It's clear that she saw Lieutenant Jervis' shadow on the wall and started screaming. Of course, she apologized later. She thought it was a strange man. It's still a bit strange, I mean, even if it was a strange man, there's no need to be so scared, right?"
[190] I pondered and nodded.
[191] Miss Lyle fell silent for a moment, then suddenly said again: "I don't know what's wrong with them this year. Everyone seems a little off. Miss Johnson always looks gloomy and doesn't want to talk; David also doesn't want to talk unless he has to; of course, Bill's mouth is still busy, but somehow, his incessant chatter seems to bother everyone more. Mr. Keller looks like a string is about to snap. They all seem to be on edge, as if - oh, I can't explain it, but it's just strange."
[192] Indeed, I thought, it's quite strange that Miss Lyle and Colonel Pennington, two very different people, could have such similar feelings.
[193] Just then, Mr. Coleman came in, literally dancing and waving his arms around, and if he had stuck out his tongue and suddenly wagged a tail at you, you wouldn't have been surprised.
[194] "Hello, hello," he said, "I am the best shopper in the world. Did you take our nurse to see the sights of the city?"
[195] "She didn't leave a good impression," Miss Lyle said dryly.
[196] "Well, that's understandable," Mr. Coleman said cheerfully, "this place is just the most run-down backwater!"
[197] "You're not the type to appreciate fancy things or antiques, are you, Bill? I can't understand why you're doing archaeological work."
[198] "It's not my fault, it's my guardian's. He's a scholar, a university researcher, the type who reads books in his pajamas. Having me as a ward must be a blow to him."
[199] "I think it's foolish to be forced to do work you don't like," the girl said sharply.
[200] "It's not forced, Shira, dear girl, I'm not forced. The old man asked me what I wanted to do with my life, and I said nothing, so he decided to send me here for a while."
[201] "But don't you know what you want to do at all? You must know!"
[202] "Of course I do. My wish is to never have to work. My favorite thing is to have lots of money and then be able to go racing."
[203] "You're ridiculous!" Miss Raeley said.
[204] She sounded very angry.
[205] "Ah, I understand it's impossible," Mr. Coleman said nonchalantly, "so I have to do something. I don't mind what I do as long as I'm not stuck in an office all day. I'd love to travel the world. As I said, we'll see, and so I came."
[206] "I'm sure you won't be of any help at all!"
[207] "You're wrong. I can stand and yell 'Allah' with anyone in the pit! And actually, my drawings aren't too bad. I was particularly good at imitating other people's handwriting, which makes me a top-notch forger. Who knows, maybe I'll go into that line of work. If you ever get splashed with mud by my Rolls-Royce while waiting for a bus, you'll know I'm on my way."
[208] "Don't you think you should be going now, instead of talking nonsense?" said Miss Raeley.
[209] "Are we very friendly here, Nurse Miss?"
[210] "I'm sure Nurse Lester is eager to settle down."
[211] "You're always so sure of everything," said Mr. Coleman, smiling.
[212] I thought to myself, "That's true. You're such an overconfident little girl."
[213] "Perhaps we should be going, Mr. Coleman," I said dryly.
[214] "Very well, Nurse Miss," replied Mr. Coleman.
[215] I shook hands with Miss Riley and thanked her, and then we left.
[216] "This Hilary, what a likable girl she is," Mr. Coleman said, "but she's always so critical."
[217] Our car drove out of the city and soon we were on a narrow, bumpy road lined with green crops.
[218] About half an hour later, Mr. Coleman pointed to a large mound on the riverbank ahead of us and said, "Yariamia ruins."
[219] I could see small black figures moving there, like ants.
[220] Just as I was looking, those people suddenly ran down the side of the mound together.
[221] "Faithful fellows," said Mr. Coleman, "it's time to quit. We quit at sundown."
[222] The archaeological camp was located near the riverbank.
[223] The driver turned the car and bumped through a narrow archway, and we arrived.
[224] The camp's houses were built around a courtyard. Initially, the rooms occupied only the southern part of the courtyard, with a few less important small houses to the east, and later the archaeological team built additional rooms on the other two sides. Since the floor plan turned out to have a special significance, I include a sketch here to illustrate it.
[225] All doors faced the courtyard, most windows did as well, with the sole exception of the initial southern rooms, which had windows facing the outside fields, although these windows were also secured from the outside with metal grilles. [234] Chapter 5
[235] Jarramiah site
[236] I must admit, my first impression of Mrs. Lidden was quite unexpected. When people talk about someone, one cannot help but imagine what they look like. I had always pictured Mrs. Lidden as a woman with dark hair, tanned skin, dissatisfied with everything, neurotic, and on edge. Frankly, I also thought she might be a bit uncouth.
[237] However, she turned out to be nothing like what I had imagined. Firstly, she was beautiful. Unlike her husband, she was not Swedish, but she looked very much like it. She had blonde hair, fair skin, and a Scandinavian appearance, which was quite rare. She was not young anymore, I guessed she was somewhere between thirty and forty years old. Her face was somewhat pale, with some gray hairs mixed in her blonde hair. But her eyes were very beautiful, the only eyes I have ever seen that could be described as purple. They were large, with a hint of shadow in them. She was very thin, looking frail. If I said she looked extremely tired but full of vitality, you would probably think I was exaggerating, but that's how I felt. Moreover, I thought she was a hundred percent a lady, which is still quite remarkable today.
[238] She extended her hand to me, smiling. Her voice was low and gentle, with a slow American accent.

[243] I liked her, and she reminded me of a nurse supervisor I had during my internship. We all admired her for her hard work.
[244] I estimated she was about fifty years old, and she had a somewhat masculine appearance. Her iron-gray hair was cut short, and she spoke in a low, staccato voice, but her voice was pleasant to hear. Her face was not pretty, with wrinkles all over, and she had a rather ridiculous hooked nose. Whenever she was upset or troubled, she would rub her nose irritably. She was dressed in a tweed suit that looked like a man's. Soon she told me she was a native of Yorkshire.
[245] Father Lavini looked a bit intimidating. He was tall, with a big black beard and a pair of spectacles with a metal frame. I had heard Mrs. Kershaw mention there was a French monk here, and the Father Lavini in front of me was wearing a white woolen monk's habit. I found it strange because I had always thought that monks would not leave the monastery after entering it.

[247] Sitting across from me were three other individuals. Mr. Littell was a plump young man with glasses. His hair was long and curly, and he had bright blue eyes. He had likely been a charming child, but now he looked rather unimpressive. In fact, he now resembled a pig. Another young man had short hair and a long, comical face with a wide grin. He had pearly white teeth and a contagious smile. However, he was quite reserved, only nodding or responding with one or two words when spoken to. Like Mr. Littell, he was also an American. The last individual was Mrs. Mukaddam. I did not have the chance to study her closely, as every time I glanced her way, she would stare at me with a hungry gaze, making me feel uneasy. If you saw the way she looked at me, you might think hospital nurses were peculiar creatures. She was completely lacking in manners.
[248] She was quite young, not exceeding twenty-five years old; her skin was dark, and she had a sneaky appearance. To put it another way, she was attractive, but perhaps as my mother often said, "touched by the tar brush" [1]. She wore a brightly colored sweater and had her nails painted the same color. Her thin, bird-like face had large eyes and a tight-lipped, suspicious expression.
[249] The tea was delicious, a rich and flavorful blend that was nothing like the mild Chinese tea Mrs. Kelsh often served. Drinking that tea was hardly enjoyable for me.
[250] The table had toast, jam, a dish of rock cakes, and sliced cake. Mr. Emott was very polite in serving me. Although he was quiet, he seemed to notice when my plate was empty and refilled it promptly.
[251] Soon, Mr. Coleman rushes in again and sits down on the other side of Miss Johnson. He seems to be in good spirits, as he starts talking non-stop as soon as he sits down.

Mr. Mocca absent-mindedly stirs his tea and says nothing, leaving his cake untouched.
[255] There is one more seat left, and soon the door opens, and a man walks in.
[256] When I first saw Richard Keller, I thought he was one of the few handsome men I had seen in a long time. But was it really so? I wasn't sure. It seemed contradictory to say someone was handsome and looked like a skull, yet that's how he appeared. Although it felt like his skin was unusually tight over his skull, the skull itself was beautiful. His chin, temples, and forehead were sharply defined, reminding me of a bronze statue. His thin, tanned face had the brightest, bluest eyes I had ever seen. He was about six feet tall, and I guessed he was under forty years old.

[271] After tea, Mrs. Lynde took me to my room.
[272] Perhaps here I should give a brief description of the arrangement of the rooms. It is very simple, and a reference to the floor plan will make it easy to understand.
[273] On either side of the open veranda there are doors leading to two main houses. The door on the right leads to the dining room, where we had tea; the other door leads to a room exactly like it (which I call the sitting room), which is used as a living room and an informal workroom. That is where some of the drawings (not only architectural) were completed, and where more delicate fragments of pottery were repaired and pieced together. Through the sitting room is the museum, where all the artifacts found at the excavation site are brought back, stored on different shelves, and some are also placed on benches or tables. There is only one way out of the museum, which goes through the sitting room, with no other exits.
[274] On the other side of the museum is Mrs. Lynde's bedroom, with a door facing the courtyard. Like the other rooms on the campsite side, the bedroom has two windows with metal grilles, facing the open countryside. Next to Mrs. Lynde's room, turning the corner, is Dr. Lynde's room, with no direct door between them. It is the first room on the east side of the camp, followed by the room prepared for me, then Miss Johnson's, Mrs. Mukada's, and Mr. Mukada's rooms. Then come two "bathrooms."
[275] (Once I mentioned "bathrooms" and Dr. Reilly heard me and laughed, saying that either it is a bathroom or it is not, there is no such thing as a "so-called bathroom." But regardless, when you get used to having a faucet and a complete plumbing system, it is strange to call these two rooms with tin bathtubs and the mud used for bathing brought in cans of kerosene "bathrooms!")

[277] Let's go back to the porch. The layout of the rooms on the other side is similar to this side. The office is connected to the dining room, where various documents are stored and cataloging and typing work are done. Corresponding to Mrs. Leidner's bedroom is Father Lavigne's room, which he was assigned the largest bedroom and also used it for translating and deciphering inscriptions, whatever you want to call it.

The dwellings of the local servants, the guardroom of the soldiers, and the stables are outside. The drawing room is on the right of the arch, occupying the rest of the north side.
[280] I have described the distribution of the rooms in detail here because I do not intend to repeat it later.

[283] "The servant will bring you hot water for lunch and dinner, and of course, in the morning as well. If you need it at any other time, just go outside and clap your hands, and when the servant comes, say 'Gibmaha,' can you remember that?"

[339] I don't like to gossip about patients, and besides, it's usually hard to get the truth from a patient's relatives. You're in the dark until you know the truth. Of course, if a doctor is responsible for the patient's treatment, he'll tell you everything you need to know. But in this case, there's no doctor in charge. They never formally invited Dr. Leary to examine her, and in my heart of hearts, I'm not entirely sure Dr. Leary told me everything he could have. I must admit, considering face-saving, husbands often hide the truth about their wives' conditions. But it doesn't matter, the more I know, the clearer it becomes what I should do. Mrs. Mukadda (I mentally decided she was a suspicious woman) seemed eager to tell me something. And putting aside professional considerations, as a human being, I also wanted to hear what she had to say. You can call it my curiosity if you like.

[345] "If it's a delusion, it shouldn't be encouraged." I said calmly.

In my opinion, there were only two possibilities: either Mrs. Mocaddo fabricated it (like a child might lie to get attention, inventing something that never happened to attract attention), or it was an intentional prank, as I mentioned earlier. That's the kind of thing a young man like Mr. Coleman might find amusing. I decided to keep a close eye on him, as a tense patient could be driven insane by a stupid joke.

[370] Mr. Mocadlo was a foolish and pitiful man, and I didn't think Mrs. Liddon would really care about his admiration, but his wife would. If I'm not mistaken, she was quite bothered by it, and would likely be delighted to retaliate against Mrs. Liddon if given the chance.

[372] Then I told myself, "Amy Lethlan, you're a fool. Mrs. Reddena isn't a child, she's almost forty, and she must know all the things that life has to teach."

[382] The bed was comfortable, but I didn't sleep well because I had many dreams.
[383] The lines of a poem by Keats kept coming to my mind, which I had to read as a child. I always got them wrong, which made me very frustrated. I used to hate that poem because I had to learn it whether I wanted to or not. However, when I woke up in the middle of the night, for some reason, I discovered its beauty for the first time in my life.
[384] "Ah, knight, tell me why you are sad, alone and helpless (what comes next?)" [1]
[385] For the first time, I saw the face of the knight in my mind. It was Mr. Keller's face, a grim, taut, copper-colored face, like the pitiful young men I saw on the battlefield in my teenage years. I felt sorry for him. Then I fell asleep again, and this time, I saw that the heartless beauty in the poem was Mrs. Riedner, leaning sideways on her horse, holding a bouquet of embroidered flowers. Suddenly, the horse stumbled and fell to the ground, revealing a field of wax-coated, bone-white corpses. I woke up in a cold sweat, trembling with fear. I had to tell myself that it was because I was not used to eating curry for dinner.

[388] Chapter Seven
[389] The Man at the Window
[390] I would like to clarify that this story is not regional. I know nothing about archaeology and do not feel inclined to learn. Spending all day with people and things buried underground seems meaningless to me. Mr. Keller once said that I lacked the temperament of an archaeologist, and he was undoubtedly right.
[391] On the morning after my arrival at the camp, Mr. Carrington asked me if I would care to see the palace he had designed – I believe he used the word "designed." I had no idea how one could design something that had existed so long ago, so I said I would, and I must confess I was rather excited. It was said to be nearly three thousand years old. I was curious to see what kind of palace people had built in those days, and what sort of furnishings it might have, like the tomb of Tutankhamun [1] that I had seen in pictures. But you may believe me, there was nothing to see but mud. There were walls about two feet high, that was all. Mr. Carrington showed me round, telling me this was the great court, and there were large meeting-rooms and other rooms of various kinds upstairs, all opening on to the court. All I could think of was, "How does he know?" Of course, I was polite enough not to ask. What I can tell you is that the visit was a complete disappointment to me. The whole excavation seemed to me nothing but mud, without marble, without gold, or anything else worth looking at. My aunt's house at Crickwood would have been more imposing if it had become a ruin. These Assyrians or whatever they were, who called themselves kings!
[392] Afterwards Mrs. Lydgate explained to me why Father Lavigne was interested in the site. She said that he was only interested in "written documents," which was her phrase. The natives wrote everything on clay tablets. They were very strange-looking, like pagans, but they were all very reasonable. They even used clay tablets in school, with the teacher's assignments on one side and the pupils' answers on the other. I must admit that this interested me, because it seemed so human, if you understand what I mean.
[393] Father Lavigne took me round the site, telling me which were temples, which were palaces, which were private houses, and one place he said was an early Akkadian cemetery. His rapid way of talking was interesting, for he only touched on each subject and then went on to the next.
[394] "It is strange that you should have come here," he said. "Is Mrs. Lydgate really ill?"
[395] "Not exactly ill," I said cautiously.
[396] He said, "She's a strange woman, and I think she's a dangerous person."
[397] "What do you mean?" I asked. "Dangerous? How so?"
[398] He thoughtfully shook his head.
[399] "I think she's a cold-blooded person," he said. "Yes, I think she can be extremely cold-blooded."
[400] "Sorry," I said, "I think you're talking nonsense."
[401] He shook his head again.
[402] "You don't understand women like I do," he said.
[403] I found this statement quite amusing coming from a monk. Of course, I suppose he may have heard many things from people's confessions, but it still puzzled me, as I wasn't sure if monks could hear confessions or if only priests could. From his long, flowing robe and the beads he wore, I assumed he was a monk.
[404] "Yes, she is heartless," he mused. "I'm certain of that. She's cold, but she still fears something. What is she afraid of?"
[405] I thought this was something we all wanted to know.
[406] At least her husband probably knows, and I don't think anyone else truly understands.
[407] He suddenly looked at me with his bright black eyes.
[408] "Isn't it strange here? Don't you find it strange? Or do you think it's normal?"
[409] "Not entirely normal," I pondered, "I find the living arrangements comfortable enough, but the atmosphere makes me uneasy."
[410] "The atmosphere unsettles me too," he suddenly became unfamiliar, "I have a feeling, something is brewing slowly. Even Dr. Laidner himself seems different, he's worried about something."
[411] "His wife's health?"
[412] "Possibly, but there's more to it. It's hard to describe, but there's an unsettling feeling here."
[413] Indeed, there is an unsettling feeling here.
[414] We didn't say much more as Dr. Laidner approached us. He showed me a recently excavated child's grave, which looked pitiful with its tiny bones and a few jars scattered around, along with some tiny, bead-like objects that Dr. Laidner told me was a string of pearls.
[415] The excavation workers made me laugh. I had never seen so many thin, ragged people gathered together. Their heads were wrapped in cloth, as if everyone had toothaches. As they carried baskets of dirt back and forth, they sang out loud, at least I thought they were singing. It was a strange, monotonous chant that repeated over and over again. I noticed that most of their eyes looked terrible, filled with discharge, and a few were almost blind. As I was about to express my pity for their fate, Dr. Laidner said to me, "Quite a nice group of people, isn't it?" I found it strange that two different people could have such opposite feelings about the same thing. I may not have expressed myself clearly, but I think you can understand what I mean.
[416] After a moment, Dr. Redner said he was going back to camp for some morning tea. So we headed back, and he gave me a tour as we walked. After his explanation, everything seemed different. I could make out where the streets and houses used to be. He even pointed out the ovens the Arabs used to bake bread and told me they were almost identical to the ones we use now.
[417] We returned to camp and found that Dr. Redner's wife was awake. She looked much better today, no longer appearing so weary and worn. Tea was served almost immediately, and Dr. Redner drank it while telling her about his morning at the dig site. He then returned to the dig site to continue working. Dr. Redner's wife asked if I wanted to see their latest discovery. Of course, I said yes, so she took me to the artifact room. It was filled with things, mostly broken jars in my opinion, along with some that were already repaired and glued back together. I thought to myself that all these things could easily be discarded as trash.
[418] "Oh, my goodness," I said, "it's a shame they're so broken, isn't it? Are they really worth preserving?"
[419] Dr. Redner's wife smiled and said, "Don't let Erik hear you say that. Pottery is more precious to him than anything else, and some of these are among our oldest artifacts, dating back nearly seven thousand years." She then went on to tell me about how some of them were discovered almost at the bottom of the mound and how people in thousands of years past had used tar to repair broken pottery. This shows that people back then valued their possessions just as much as we do now.
[420] "Now," she said, "I'm going to show you something even more exciting."
[421] "Now," she said, "I'm going to show you something even more exciting."
[422] She took down a box from the shelf, and inside was a beautiful golden dagger with a deep blue gem on the handle.
[423] I couldn't help but exclaim in delight.
[424] Dr. Redna's wife smiled.
[425] "Everyone likes gold, except my husband."
[426] "Ah, first of all, because it's too expensive. You have to pay the diggers who find the artifacts an equal weight of gold."
[427] "My goodness!" I exclaimed, "Why?"
[428] "Oh, it's a tradition. One reason is to prevent them from stealing. You see, if they really did steal it, it wouldn't be because of its archaeological value, but because of the value of the gold itself. They would melt it down. By doing this, we can more easily ensure their honesty."
[429] She took down another tray and showed me a very beautiful gold goblet with a ram's head pattern on it.
[430] I exclaimed again.
[431] "You see, it's very pretty, isn't it? These were found in a prince's tomb. We've also discovered other royal tombs, but most have already been looted. This goblet is our best find and one of the most beautiful artifacts ever excavated. It's unique and used by the early Akkadians."
[432] Suddenly, Mrs. Liddell frowned and brought the cup closer to examine it, carefully scraping it with her nails.
[433] "Strange! There's wax on it. Someone must have brought a candle in." She scraped off the small piece of wax and put the cup back in its place.
[434] Then, she showed me several strange small clay sculptures, most of which I found vulgar. I must say, it's hard to believe that the minds of these ancient people could be so crude.
[435] When we returned to the veranda, we saw Mrs. McCardle sitting there painting her nails. She held her hands up to admire her work. I couldn't help but think that there's no color more unattractive than this orange-red.
[436] Mrs. Laidner brought out a broken, exquisite little dish from the artifact room, trying to glue it back together. I watched for a few minutes and then asked if I could help.
[437] "Oh, of course, there are many more." She brought over a pile of broken pottery pieces, and we got to work. I quickly found the trick, and she complimented me on my talent. I suppose nurses usually have nimble fingers.
[438] "Everyone is so busy!" Mrs. Mocadjo said. "It makes me feel so idle, of course, I am idle."
[439] "If you like being idle, what's wrong with that?" Mrs. Laidner said.
[440] Her voice sounded very cold.
[441] At noon, we had lunch. After lunch, Dr. Liddon and Mr. Macadoo began to clean the pottery. They poured diluted hydrochloric acid on them, and one of the jars turned into a beautiful purple, while the other showed a pattern of bull's horns. It was amazing. All the hard-to-clean dry mud turned into foam when diluted hydrochloric acid was poured on it, and it was easy to wash off.
[442] Mr. Kari and Mr. Coleman went back to the dig site, and Mr. Leiter went into the photography room.

[451] Of course, I gave up arguing. As I left to take a brief rest, I suddenly felt that Mrs. Ladden was odd. Despite her strong anxiety, she wanted to go for a walk without any protection.
[452] At 3:30 p.m., when I stepped out of my room, the courtyard was empty except for a small boy washing pottery with a large copper basin, and Mr. Emott was sorting and arranging them nearby. As I approached them, Mrs. Ladden walked in from the archway, looking more lively than I had seen her before. Her eyes sparkled, and she seemed spirited and almost joyful.
[453] Dr. Ladden emerged from the laboratory to greet her, holding out a large platter with a bull's horn pattern.

[455] "Even with what we've already found, he hasn't figured out much," Mrs. Ladden said coldly. "He may be a good epigrapher, but he's also a very lazy man, and he spends his afternoons sleeping."

[457] After tea, Mrs. Laidner asked me if I would like to go for a walk by the river with her. I think she may have been worried that my refusal of her company earlier in the afternoon might have hurt my feelings.
[458] I wanted to let her know that I'm not a petty person, so I immediately agreed.
[459] It was a beautiful evening, and a small path led through the fields of wheat, then past fruit trees in bloom. We finally arrived at the banks of the Tigris River. On our left was the excavation site, and the workers were still singing their strange, monotonous songs. On our right was a watermill, emitting a strange, moaning sound. At first, this sound made me uneasy, but as time went on, I grew to like it, as it seemed to have a soothing effect on me. On the other side of the watermill was a village where most of the workers lived.

[482] Chapter Eight
[483] Nightmare
[484] During the week after I arrived at the Yariya ruins, it was difficult to pinpoint exactly what things I should pay attention to.
[485] Looking back at the situation as I now understand it, I can see many clues that I did not notice at the time. However, in order to make this story more plausible, I should still try to recapture the feelings I had then – confusion, unease, and an increasingly strong sense that something was not right.
[486] First and foremost, there is one thing that is undeniably true: the strange, tense atmosphere is not just imagined, but very real. Even someone as slow-witted as Bill Coleman has commented on it.
[487] "This place makes me feel uneasy," I once heard him say, "Are these people always so gloomy?"
[488] This is what he said to another assistant, David Emott. I have grown to like Mr. Emott, and I am sure his quiet demeanor is not meant to be hostile. He seems to have a steadfast and reassuring air about him, especially in a situation where everyone is unsure of each other's feelings and thoughts.
[489] "No," he answered Mr. Coleman, "it was not like that last year."

[492] Mr. Emott shrugged and said nothing.
[493] I had a very enlightening conversation with Miss Johnson. I liked her, she was capable, intelligent, and practical. It was evident that she greatly admired Dr. Redden.
[494] This time she told me about his life story from his youth. She knew about every site he had excavated and all the findings. I almost bet she could quote every word he said in his speeches. She told me that she believed Dr. Redden was the greatest archaeologist in the world today.
[495] "And he's so innocent, completely unaware of the ways of the world and the pride that comes with it. Only truly great people can be so innocent."

Richard Carey and he worked together in Palestine for almost ten years. Oh, I've known him for seven years as well."
[498] "Mr. Carey is so handsome," I said.
[499] "Yes, I suppose so," she answered briefly.

[503] "Since…" I prompted.

[509] It seemed that Miss Johnson and Mrs. Kelsey shared the same opinion that Mrs. Redman was the cause of the tense atmosphere. But how could we explain her own anxiety and fear?

If coming to such a remote place makes her feel tense, she should have stayed in America. I can't stand people who come to a place and do nothing but complain!"
[511] Following this, perhaps worried that she had said too much and might have misunderstood her intentions, she continued: "Of course, I like her. She's a very charming person. She could be quite alluring if she wanted to."
[512] The topic then came to an abrupt end.
[513] I thought to myself, it's always like this. Whenever women are put together, they inevitably develop jealousy. It's clear that Miss Johnson doesn't like her boss's wife (which is understandable), and unless I'm completely mistaken, I think Mrs. McAdoo hates Mrs. Laiden.

[562] Chapter 9
[563] Dr. Lieden's wife's story
[564] After lunch, Dr. Lieden's wife retired to her room for a rest as usual. I put her to bed with several pillows and her favorite books. As I was about to leave the room, she called me back.

Just a few months after our marriage, I discovered by accident that my husband was a spy working for Germany. I learned that the information he provided led to the sinking of an American transport ship and the death of hundreds of people. I don't know what most people would do in such a situation, but I'll tell you what I did. My father worked at the U.S. Army Department, so I went straight to him and told him the truth. Frederick did die in the war, but he was executed as a spy in the United States."

[599] Her voice was filled with pain, and I nodded sympathetically.
[600] "Many men wanted to marry me, but I turned them down. This incident was too much for me, and it made me feel that I couldn't trust anyone."
[601] "Hmm, I can imagine how that feels."

[604] "Yes. At first, I thought I was crazy or dreaming. Then I went to my father, and he told me the truth. My husband had not been executed but had escaped. However, he eventually couldn't escape his fate. A few weeks later, a train accident occurred, and his body was found among the victims. My father had kept his escape a secret from me, and since he was already dead, he thought it unnecessary to tell me these things until I went to him.
[605] "But with the appearance of that letter, the whole situation had a new possibility. Could it be that my husband was still alive?"
[606] "My father did his best to investigate this matter. He claimed that he could believe, within his reach, that the body buried as Frederick was indeed Frederick himself. [607] "The same thing happened again and again, and I received a threatening letter every time I got close to a man."
[608] "Is it in your husband's handwriting?"
[609] She slowly said: "It's hard to say. I didn't keep his previous letters, so I can only judge by memory."

[613] "There is one possibility. Frederick had a younger brother named William, who was only ten years old when we got married, at most twelve. [678] Chapter 10

[849] "Yes, it does seem that way," Dr. Riley said thoughtfully, looking at Captain Melton. "Coincidence, huh? What do you think, Captain Melton? Do you agree with this idea? Should we bring this up with Lydena?"

[852] "Have you heard of a man named Hercule Poirot, Dr. Redner?"
[853] Dr. Redner looked at him, puzzled.
[854] "Yes, I think I've heard of that name," he said blankly. "I once heard Mr. Van Alden mention it, with quite high praise. He's a private detective, isn't he?"
[855] "That's the man."
[856] "But he should be in London, what use could he be to us?"

[867] "Of course not." Dr. Riddern said, his lips twitching with pain. "So, Dr. Lyle, would you be willing to represent me and speak with this Mr. Hercule Poirot?"
[868] "I would."
[869] Dr. Riddern gestured his appreciation.

[871] I could not hold back any longer.

[883] Chapter 13
[884] Hercule Poirot's Arrival
[885] I'll never forget the feeling I had when I first saw Hercule Poirot. Of course, I got used to him eventually, but at first, it was quite a shock, and I'm sure anyone else would have felt the same.
[886] I had no idea what he should look like in my imagination, perhaps a little like Sherlock Holmes, tall and thin, with a clever, sharp face. Of course, I knew he was a foreigner, but I didn't expect him to look so foreign, if you understand what I mean.
[887] When you saw him, you couldn't help but laugh. He was like a character from a stage or a picture, standing in front of you. He was no more than five feet five inches tall. To me, he looked like a strange, plump little man, quite old, with a long, bushy mustache and an egg-shaped head, like a comic-opera barber!
[888] And this was the man who was going to help us find the killer of Mrs. Ledeau!
[889] I suppose I showed some distaste, for he turned on me at once, with a strange light in his eyes, and said, "You don't like me, Nurse Miss? Remember, you don't know if the pudding is good until you've tasted it."
[890] I think he meant that words are no proof, only facts count.
[891] However, this statement is absolutely correct, but I dare not say I have much confidence in him!
[892] Soon after Sunday lunch, Dr. Lyle brought him in his own car, and the first thing he did was to make us all gather together.

[1337] Chapter Seventeen
[1338] Stains on the washstand
[1339] Dr. Laidner's body had been sent to Hashani for the autopsy, and the room remained untouched except for that. There was little to see in the room, and the police did not take long to search it.
[1340] Upon entering, the bed was to the right, the two windows facing the outside fields and fitted with bars. Between the two windows was a plain oak table with two drawers, which Dr. Laidner used as a dressing table. There was a row of hooks on the east wall, with cotton cloth bags covering the clothes hanging there, and a pine chest of drawers. The washstand was to the left of the door. In the center of the room was a large, plain oak table with blotting paper, ink bottles, and a small suitcase. The anonymous letters received by Dr. Laidner were kept in this suitcase. The curtains were short, with a white background and orange stripes, made of local fabric. The stone floor was covered with several goat-skin rugs, with three narrow brown-and-white striped ones in front of the two windows and the washstand, and a larger, better-quality white-and-brown striped one between the bed and the writing desk.
[1341] There were no cabinets, no niches in the walls, no long curtains, and in fact, no place for anyone to hide. The bed was an ordinary iron bed with a printed cotton cover. The only luxury in the room was the three pillows, made of the softest down feathers. Only Mrs. Liddon had such pillows.
[1342] Dr. Leary briefly explained where Mrs. Liddon's body was found - on the carpet by the bed, curled up.
[1343] To further clarify, he indicated that I should walk over.

[1380] "Unless what?"
[1381] Dr. Leroy slowly said, "There is also the possibility that Mrs. Laiden was kneeling on the ground, in which case, using a heavy object to strike from above, it would not require much force."
[1382] "Kneeling," Poirot thought, "that is also a possibility..."
[1383] "Take note, this is just an idea," the doctor pointed out quickly, "there is no evidence to support this."
[1384] "But it is possible."
[1385] "Yes. And don't forget, if we consider the circumstances at the time, it was not an incredible thing. When her instinct told her it was too late and no one could arrive in time, out of fear, she could have chosen to kneel and beg for mercy instead of shouting for help."
[1386] "Yes," Poirot mused, "it is an idea..."
[1387] I find this idea quite far-fetched, as I cannot imagine Mrs. Leroy kneeling to anyone.
[1388] Poirot slowly looked around the room. He opened the windows, checked the bars, and tried to stick his head out. When he found that, no matter what method he used, his shoulders could not extend out with his head, he seemed satisfied.
[1389] "You found her with the windows closed," he said, "so were they closed when you left her at 12:45?"
[1390] "Yes, the windows are usually closed in the afternoon. Unlike the windows in the living room and dining room, these windows do not have window screens, and they can be closed to prevent flies from coming in."
[1391] "And in any case, it is impossible for an outsider to get in there," Poirot pondered. "The walls are made of the strongest mud bricks, with no secret doors or skylights. No, the only way to enter this room is through the door; and the only way to reach the door is through the courtyard; and the courtyard has only one entrance, the arch. There are five people outside the arch, and their stories are consistent, and I do not think they are lying… Yes, they are not lying. The killer was here…"
[1392] I said nothing. Hadn't I felt the same way just now when we were sitting around the table?
[1393] Poirot continued to slowly pace around the room. He picked up a photo from the dresser, which showed an old man with a white goatee. He looked at me inquiringly.
[1394] "That's Mrs. Lendon's father," I said. "She told me herself."
[1395] He put down the photo and began to browse through the items on the vanity. They were ordinary tortoise shell items, simple but of good quality. He looked up at the row of books on the bookshelf and read the titles aloud.
[1396] "The Greeks Unveiled, An Introduction to Relativity, The Life of Lady Stanhope, The Kruger, The Thousand Years Man, Linda Condon. Ah, perhaps these books can tell us something. Mrs. Laidner is no fool, she's a smart woman."
[1397] "Oh! She's a very clever woman," I said eagerly. "Very well-read and knowledgeable, not at all a mediocre person."
[1398] He looked at me appraisingly and smiled.
[1399] "She's certainly not mediocre," he said. "I've already found out."
[1400] He continued to examine the room. He paused for a moment in front of the washstand, which was cluttered with bottles and jars, as well as various face creams.
[1401] Then he suddenly knelt down and began to examine the carpet.
[1402] Dr. Lyle and I immediately went over to see him examining a small, dark brown stain. Almost imperceptible on the brown carpet, the stain was only noticeable because a portion of it had spread onto the white stripes.
[1403] "What do you think it is, doctor?" he asked, "Blood?"
[1404] Dr. Lyle also knelt down.
[1405] "It could be," he said, "if you wish, I can find out for you."
[1406] "That would be greatly appreciated."
[1407] Mr. Poirot continued to examine the water jug and washbasin. The water jug was placed on one side of the washbasin stand, and the washbasin was empty, but a empty kerosene can was placed beside it to hold dirty water.
[1408] He turned to me.
[1409] "Nurse, do you remember whether the water jug was in the washbasin or outside of it when you left Mrs. Laidlaw at 12:45?"
[1410] "I'm not entirely sure," I thought for a moment. "I think it was inside the washbasin."
[1411] "Ah?"
[1412] "But you know," I added hastily, "I just thought so because it usually is. The servants put it there after lunch. I just thought I would have noticed if it wasn't."
[1413] He nodded approvingly.
[1414] "Yes, I understand that. That's the training you received in the hospital. If something is out of place in a patient's room, you might unconsciously put it back without even noticing. So, was it in the same position after the murder as it is now?"
[1415] I shook my head.
[1416] "I didn't notice," I said. "I was just looking around to see if there was anywhere to hide someone or if the killer had left anything behind."
[1417] "It must be blood," Dr. Leigh said, standing up. "Is that important?"
[1418] Poirot frowned, spreading his hands in exasperation.
[1419] "I don't know, how could I know? It could mean nothing. I think the killer touched her and got blood on his hands. Just a little bit, but still blood, so he came here to wash it off. [1420] "It must be just a little bit of blood," Dr. Leigh said uncertainly. "No blood spatter or anything like that. Maybe just a little bit of blood seeping out from the wound. Of course, if he had checked...
[1421] I shuddered. A revolting image flashed through my mind. I saw a man, perhaps the cheerful young photographer with the piggy face, bringing this charming woman to the ground, then leaning over her with satisfaction, checking the wound with his fingers; perhaps, his face would be completely different... full of cruelty and madness...
[1422] Dr. Raley noticed me shuddering.
[1423] "What's wrong, Nurse Miss?" he asked.
[1424] "Nothing, just goosebumps," I said. "Like a goose walked through my grave."
[1425] Mr. Poirot turned to look at me.
[1426] "I know what you need," he said. "Once we've finished here, I'll go back to Hercule with the doctor. We'll take you with us. You'll have tea with Nurse Lestrade, won't you, doctor?"
[1427] "Certainly."
[1428] "Oh, no, doctor," I protested. "I couldn't possibly."
[1429] Mr. Poirot friendly patted my shoulder. It was a very British pat, not at all foreign.
[1430] "Nurse, just do as I say," he said. "And it will be good for me too. There are many things I want to discuss, but not here, because here everyone must maintain propriety. This excellent Dr. Reddarn adores his wife and believes, with great certainty, that everyone else thinks the same about her. But in my opinion, that's not human nature! No, we want - how do you say it - to have an open discussion about Mrs. Reddarn. That's settled. Once we've finished here, we'll take you to Hercule."
[1431] "I feel," I hesitated, "I should leave here regardless. It's becoming too awkward to stay."
[1432] "First, do not act hastily for the next couple of days," said Dr. Raley. "It would not be appropriate to leave before the funeral."
[1433] "You make it sound easy," I said, "What if I were murdered, doctor?"
[1434] I said this sentence half-jokingly, and I believe Dr. Riley would understand it that way too, and likely respond with a joke as well.
[1435] However, what surprised me was that Mr. Poirot stood motionless in the middle of the room, with his head in his hands.
[1436] "Ah! If that's possible," he muttered, "then it's dangerous. Yes, very dangerous. What can we do? How can we protect ourselves?"
[1437] "Oh, Mr. Poirot," I said, "I was just joking! I wonder who would want to kill me?"
[1438] "Not only you, but others as well," he said, but I didn't like the tone of his voice. It was too eerie.
[1439] "But why?" I asked.
[1440] He looked straight at me.
[1441] "I also like to joke, Miss," he said, "and I can laugh. But some things are not jokes. My profession has taught me some things. One of them, and the most terrible one, is that murder is a habit…"

[1443] [1] British proverb. It was believed that goosebumps were caused by a goose walking over the spot where one would be buried.
[1444] Chapter Eighteen
[1445] Tea at Dr. Leroy's house

[1447] These questions were mainly about the stranger's appearance, the one who had peered into the window and spoken to Father Lavigne the next day.

[1542] "It's not right to speak ill of the dead, but I think it's foolish to pretend that she wasn't the way she was. She was a woman who liked to ruin things for the fun of it – or to feel powerful – or just because she was born that way. [1576] Chapter Nineteen
[1577] New Suspicions
[1578] We couldn't say much more, as just then Dr. Rheinardt came in, joking that he had just killed his most troublesome patient.
[1579] He and Mr. Poirot sat down and engaged in a nearly professional discussion about the psychological characteristics and mental state of the anonymous letter writer. The doctor referred to a few cases he had encountered in his career, while Mr. Poirot shared some of his own experiences.
[1580] "Sometimes it's not as simple as it seems," he concluded, "with a controlling nature and often a strong sense of inferiority."
[1581] Dr. Raley nodded.
[1582] "That's why you'll find that the person who wrote the anonymous letter is often the one you least suspect. These quiet, timid, obviously unassuming individuals, who appear charming and likable with a Christian-like humility, are filled with hellish rage within!"
[1583] Mr. Poirot pondered, "Do you think Mrs. Ledoux has any inferiority complex?"
[1584] Dr. Raley emptied his pipe and chuckled.
[1585] "If anyone had an inferiority complex, it would be the last one to be suspected. She shows no signs of emotional repression. She wants more life experiences, and she has gotten them!"
[1586] "Do you think, from a psychological perspective, she could have written those letters?"
[1587] "It's possible, but even if she did, it would be due to her desire for attention. Mrs. Laidner is like a movie star in her personal life. She must be in the spotlight, the center of attention. So, according to the law of complementarity, she eventually married Dr. Laidner, who is the most unassuming and modest person I've ever known. He adores her, but that adoration from family isn't enough for her. She wants to be a persecuted heroine."
[1588] "Actually," Poirot smiles, "you don't agree with his theory. You don't think she wrote those letters and then forgot about them, do you?"
[1589] "No, I don't agree. But I didn't argue with him face-to-face. You can't tell a man who has just lost his beloved wife that she was actually shameless and attention-seeking. You can't tell him that she made him worry sick just to satisfy her desire for attention. It's too risky to tell a man the truth about his wife. Yet, ironically, I can trust women. I can have a frank conversation with them about their husbands. Even if the man is a scoundrel, a liar, a drug addict, a habitual liar, or a lecher, women will accept it without batting an eye and won't let it affect their feelings for that scoundrel."
[1590] "Dr. Reilly, honestly, what is your opinion on Mrs. Laidner?"

[2644] "First of all, one suspect has been eliminated, and that is Miss Johnson herself, because I don't even consider the possibility of suicide.
[2645] "Now let's examine the facts surrounding the second murder."
[2646] "Fact one: On Saturday night, Nurse Lestrange found Miss Johnson crying. On the same evening, Miss Johnson burned a fragment of a letter, and Nurse Lestrange believed that the handwriting on the letter was the same as the anonymous letters.
[2647] "Fact two: On the night before Miss Johnson's death, Nurse Lestrange found her standing on the roof. Using Nurse Lestrange's description, she was in an incredible state of fear. When Nurse Lestrange asked her, she said, 'I saw how someone could come in from the outside, and no one could guess.' She didn't want to say anything more. Father Lavigne was passing through the courtyard, and Mr. Rutter was standing at the door of the photography room.
[2648] "Fact three: When Miss Johnson was found, she was on the verge of death, and the only thing she could say clearly was 'that window - that window -'.
[2649] "These are the facts, and here is the problem we face:
[2650] "What is the truth about the anonymous letters?"
[2651] "What did Miss Johnson see on the roof?
[2652] "What does she mean by 'that window... that window'?
[2653] "Let's start with the second easiest problem to solve. Nurse Lestrange and I climbed onto the roof, and I stood where Miss Johnson had stood. From there, she could see the courtyard, the archway, the rooms to the north of the camp, and the two members of the archaeological team. Does what she said have anything to do with Mr. Lethbridge or Father Lavine?
[2654] "Almost immediately, a possible explanation came to my mind. If a stranger came in from the outside, he would have to disguise himself. And the only person here whose appearance could be disguised is Father Lavine! With a hard hat, sunglasses, a black beard glued on, and wearing a monk's woolen robe, a stranger could walk in and not be noticed by the servants.
[2655] "Is that what Miss Johnson meant? Or did she have a deeper meaning? Did she realize that Father Lavine was an imposter? Did she know he was someone else entirely?
[2656] "After learning about Father Lavigne, I thought the mystery was solved. Raul Monier was the killer. He killed Madame Ledoux to keep her from revealing his identity. Now that someone else had seen through his secret, she had to be eliminated too.
[2657] "So everything made sense! The second murder. Father Lavigne's escape - of course, after removing his robe and shaving off his beard. (He and his friend probably had two perfect business traveler passports and were speeding through Syria.) He also placed the blood-stained handkerchief under Miss Johnson's bed.
[2658] "As I said, I was quite satisfied, but not completely. Because a perfect answer should be able to explain everything, and this one couldn't.
[2659] "For example, it couldn't explain why Miss Johnson said 'that window' when she was dying; why she suddenly cried over the letters; why she was so unbelievably frightened on the roof; or why she refused to tell Nurse Lacey what she suspected or knew.
[2660] "This answer fits the surface facts, but it doesn't satisfy the psychological needs of the mystery.
[2661] "So, as I stood on the roof, thinking over the anonymous letters, the roof, and the window, I saw it! Just as Miss Johnson had seen it!
[2662] "And this time, what I saw could explain everything!"
