[2666] Chapter 28
[2667] Journey's End
[2668] Poirot looked around. Every eye was now on him. The tension that had eased a moment ago returned with a vengeance.
[2669] There was something important to be announced... something important...
[2670] Poirot continued in his calm and composed voice: "The anonymous letter, the roof, 'the windows'... Yes, everything can be explained, and it all fits together perfectly.
[2671]"I have just said that there were three people who had an alibi at the time of the crime. I have already explained that two of them were not reliable. Now I have seen my own colossal, staggering mistake. The third alibi is worthless. Dr. Laidner is not only capable of committing the murder, but I am convinced that the real killer is him."
[2672]There was a moment of silence in the room, a silence of bewilderment and confusion. Dr. Laidner said nothing. He seemed still lost in his own faraway world. It was David Emmott who spoke first, awkwardly shifting in his seat.
[2673]"Mr. Poirot, I don't understand what you're implying. I told you that Dr. Laidner had never left the roof before three o'clock. That's a fact. I swear to you, I'm not lying. And he couldn't possibly have come down from the roof without me seeing him."
[2674]Poirot nodded.
[2675]"Yes, I believe you. Dr. Laidner did not leave the roof. That is a fact. But what I have seen, and what Miss Johnson has seen, is that Dr. Laidner could have killed his wife without leaving the roof."
[2676] We were all speechless.
[2677] "The window," Poirot exclaimed loudly. "Her window! That's what I realized, and it's exactly what Miss Johnson realized. Her window is right below, on the other side of the yard. And Dr. Laidner was up there alone, where no one could see what he was doing. The heavy millstones were all there, within reach. So simple, so very simple, if one assumes that the killer had the opportunity to move the body... ah, such a simple yet elegant solution!"
[2678] "Listen, this is what happened:
[2679] "Dr. Laidner was working on his pottery on the roof. He called you up, Mr. Emott, and when he was talking to you, he noticed, as he does every time, that the boy would put down his work and sneak out to the yard when you were not around. So he kept you up there for ten minutes and then sent you down. Just as you went down to call the boy, he began his plan.
[2680] "He took out the clay-covered mask from his pocket – the one he used to scare his wife before, and this time he lowered it with a rope from the edge of the parapet until it could lightly touch his wife's window."
[2681] "Remember, it was the window facing the field, opposite the yard.
[2682] "Mrs. Laiden was lying in bed, dozing peacefully and happily. Suddenly, the mask began to knock on the window, which caught her attention. But it wasn't twilight, it was broad daylight, so it wasn't frightening. She recognized the mask and realized what was going on - it was a silly prank! She stopped being afraid and became angry. So she did what any other woman in her situation would do - she got out of bed, opened the window, and stuck her head out between the bars to see who was playing a trick on her.
[2683] "Dr. Laiden was waiting there. He had a heavy hand mill in his hand, all set. As soon as the opportunity arose, he threw it down...
[2684] "Mrs. Laiden only managed to utter a faint cry (which Miss Johnson heard) before collapsing on the carpet below the window.
[2685] "The hand mill had a hole in it, and Dr. Laiden had already threaded a rope through it. All he had to do now was pull on the rope to pull the hand mill up. Then he turned the hand mill so that the bloodied side was facing down and arranged it neatly with the other things on the roof.
[2686] "He continued working for an hour or more, until he thought he could take the next step. He came down the stairs, spoke to Mr. Emmott and Nurse Lasher, went through the yard, and entered his wife's room. Here is his own account of what he did in that room:
[2687] "I saw my wife's body curled up beside the bed. For a moment I felt paralyzed. Then I was able to walk over to her and kneel down beside her, lifting her head. I saw that she was dead... Finally, I got up, feeling dizzy and drunk. I managed to get to the door and call for someone to come in."
[2688] "For a man distraught with grief, this was a plausible account of his actions. But now let me tell you what I believe to be the true facts. Dr. Ladden went into the room, quickly went to the window, put on a pair of gloves, closed and latched the window, then lifted his wife's body and placed it between the bed and the door. Then he noticed a small bloodstain on the carpet by the window. He couldn't replace it with another carpet because it didn't fit, but he could do the next best thing. He put the bloodstained carpet in front of the washstand, and the washstand's carpet in front of the window. If the bloodstain was discovered, it would be associated with the washstand, not the window, which was crucial. It couldn't be linked to the crime scene. He then went to the door and played the part of the distraught husband. I think it wasn't difficult for him, as he truly loved his wife."
[2689] "My dear fellow," Dr. Riley exclaimed impatiently, "if he loved her, why did he kill her? What was his motive? Dr. Ladden, can't you say something? Tell this man he's crazy."
[2690] Dr. Ladden neither spoke nor moved.
[2691] "Have I not been telling you all along that this was a case of a love affair gone wrong? Why did her first husband, Frederick Bosna, threaten to kill her? Because he loved her... And you see, in the end, he carried out his threat..."
[2692] "And so, when I finally realized that the killer was Dr. Lerne, everything fell into place..."
[2693] "So, let us start again, this time from the beginning of Dr. Lerne's first marriage to Mrs. Lerne, to the time she received the threatening letters, to her second marriage. Those letters had prevented her from marrying any other man, but they had not stopped her from marrying Dr. Lerne. How simple it would have been if Dr. Lerne had been Frederick Bosna all along."
[2694] "So, let us put ourselves in the shoes of the young Frederick Bosna. First, he loved his wife, Louis, with an irresistible, all-consuming love that only a woman like her could inspire. But she betrayed him, he was sentenced to death, and he escaped. Then he met with a train accident that allowed him to transform himself into Erik Lerne, while the real Erik Lerne, a young Swedish archaeologist, met a tragic end in the accident. Due to the severe disfigurement of the body, it was easy to mistake him for Frederick Bosna and bury him as such."
[2695] "And so, my dear friends, we have come full circle. The mystery has been solved."
[2696] "I believe Dr. Laidner wanted to ensure that everything was foolproof. The intimacy of married life might awaken a dormant memory, and he wanted to leave his wife with the impression that Eric Laidner and Frederick Bosner were two completely different people. So he needed to write a threatening letter for the latter. Then came the somewhat foolish gas poisoning incident, of course, also arranged by Dr. Laidner for the same purpose.
[2697] "After that, he was satisfied. There was no need for more letters, and they could finally settle down and live their happy married life together.
[2698] "But why did another letter come?
[2699] "I think Dr. Laidner wanted to make sure everything was foolproof. The intimacy of married life might awaken a dormant memory, and he wanted to leave his wife with the impression that Eric Laidner and Frederick Bosner were two completely different people. So he needed to write a threatening letter for the latter. Then came the somewhat foolish gas poisoning incident, of course, also arranged by Dr. Laidner for the same purpose.
[2700] "After that, he was satisfied. There was no need for more letters, and they could finally settle down and live their happy married life together.
[2701] "Then, almost two years later, the threatening letters reappear.
[2702] "Why? Well, I think I know the reason. The threats in the letters have always been genuine threats. (This is also the reason why Mrs. Lundner has always been genuinely afraid.) Once she belongs to any other man, he will kill her. And now she has fallen in love with Richard Carey.
[2703] "Similarly, after discovering this, Dr. Lundner begins to coldly and ruthlessly plan the murder.
[2704] "Now do you understand the important role that Nurse Lasher plays in all of this? Dr. Lundner must ensure that she is the one taking care of his wife, and this strange behavior (which has puzzled me from the beginning) can now be explained. Finding a reliable and knowledgeable witness who can unequivocally confirm that Mrs. Lundner's body had been dead for over an hour when it was found is crucial. That way, everyone can swear that her husband was on the roof when she was killed. Because perhaps someone would suspect that he killed her after entering the room and then pretended to discover the body. But if a nurse who has been trained in a hospital clearly states that she had been dead for an hour, that problem doesn't exist.
[2705] "Another thing that can be explained is the strange, tense atmosphere that has been present in the archaeological team this year. From the beginning, I didn't think it was just because of Mrs. Lundner's influence. For years, this archaeological team has been known for its close-knit members, and in my opinion, the psychological state of a team is usually directly influenced by its leader. Dr. Lundner, although quiet, has a strong personality. It is also because of his quick thinking, his discernment, and his approachability that the team's atmosphere has always been harmonious."
[2706] "Therefore, if the atmosphere changed, the change must have been caused by the leader, that is, Dr. Ladden. It was not Mrs. Ladden, but Dr. Ladden who should be held responsible for the tense and uneasy atmosphere. It's no wonder that the team members felt the change but didn't know the reason. The seemingly unchanged and amiable Dr. Ladden was just playing a role, while the real one was immersed in his murder plan.
[2707] "Now let's look at the second murder case - the death of Miss Johnson. She was definitely cleaning Dr. Ladden's files in the office (a task she volunteered for and took on because she wanted something to do) when she accidentally discovered an unfinished draft of an anonymous letter.
[2708] "This was undoubtedly both incomprehensible and heartbreaking for her! It turned out that Dr. Ladden had been deliberately threatening his wife! She couldn't understand the reason behind it, but she was still overwhelmed with sadness. It was in this state of mind that she couldn't help but cry, and Nurse Lasher found her.
[2709] "I don't think she suspected Dr. Ladden at that time, but my experiments in the rooms of Mrs. Ladden and Father Lavigne did give her some clues. She realized that if she heard Mrs. Ladden's scream, the window in her room must have been open instead of closed. At that time, this seemed unimportant to her, but it left an impression.
[2710] "Her mind never stopped thinking, trying to uncover the truth. Perhaps she mentioned the letters, which made Dr. Ladden realize his attitude had changed. She could see that he suddenly became afraid."
[2711] "But Dr. Laidner couldn't have killed his wife! He was on the roof the whole time.
[2712] "Then one evening, as she stood on the roof, pondering, she suddenly had an epiphany. Mrs. Laidner was killed from this roof, through the open window.
[2713] "It was at that moment that Nurse Latham found her.
[2714] "In an instant, her old feelings resurfaced, and she quickly came up with an excuse. She couldn't let Nurse Latham guess the shocking fact she had just discovered.
[2715] "She deliberately looked in the opposite direction (towards the yard), just as Father Lavine walked through the yard, and she took advantage of the situation to say that sentence."
[2716] "She refused to say more, only that she had to 'think it over thoroughly.'
[2717] "Meanwhile, Dr. Laidner was growing increasingly anxious. He knew she had discovered the truth. She was not the type of woman to hide her fear and sorrow from him.
[2718] "After all, she hadn't revealed his secret yet, but how long could he trust her?
[2719] "Murder is a habit. He replaced the water she had on her nightstand with a solution of hydrochloric acid. Some might think she had intentionally taken poison, or even that she had committed the first murder and now regretted it. To reinforce the latter idea, he also took the hand mill from the roof and placed it under her bed.
[2720] "It's no wonder Miss Johnson was so desperate to reveal what she had learned before she died. The method of killing Mrs. Laidner through 'that window' – not through the door, but through the window…"
[2721] "So far, everything could be explained, the truth was out…perfect from a psychological point of view."
[2722] "But there was no evidence…no evidence at all…"
[2723] No one spoke. We were still in a state of extreme shock…yes, and not only shock, but also pity and sympathy.
[2724] Dr. Redding did not move or speak. He had sat there all along, looking like an exhausted and weary old man.
[2725] Finally, he stirred and looked at Poirot with his gentle and weary eyes.
[2726] "Yes," he said, "there's no evidence. But that doesn't matter. You know I won't deny the facts... I never deny the facts... I think—actually—I'm quite relieved... because I'm so tired..."
[2727] Then he added succinctly: "I'm sorry, Anne. I did a wrong thing, a stupid thing, not on purpose! Poor Anne, she suffered too. Yes, not on purpose. Just because I was afraid..."
[2728] A faint smile curved his lips, twisted by pain.
[2729] "Mr. Poirot, you could have been an excellent archaeologist. You have a talent for recreating the past. Everything was just as you said.
[2730] "I loved Louise, and I killed her... if you had known Louise, you would understand... oh no, I think—no matter what—you already understand..."
[2731] Chapter 29
[2732] Epilogue
[2733] There's not much to add.
[2734] The Reverend Lavine and his accomplices were arrested just as they were about to board the ship in Beirut.
[2735] Hilary Rale married the young Emott. I think it's for the best. Emott isn't a pushover, he'll keep her in line. If she had married poor Bill Coleman, she wouldn't have been kind to him.
[2736] By the way, a year ago, when Coleman had an appendicitis attack, I took care of him, and I grew to like him. His family was planning to send him to a farm in South Africa.
[2737] Since then, I have not been to the East again. Interestingly, sometimes I do miss it. I can hear the sound of the water wheel, the women washing clothes, and the strange proud look of the camel looking at you, which makes me nostalgic. After all, the earth may not be as harmful to health as we have been taught since childhood.
[2738] Dr. Lyle often visits me when he comes to Britain. As I said, I wouldn't have gotten into this mess if it weren't for him. "Take it or leave it," I told him. "I know there are a lot of grammar mistakes and it's not well-written, but that's all I can do."
[2739] He took it without hesitation. If it were published, I would find it strange.
[2740] Mr. Polo first returned to Syria and then took the Orient Express back to Britain a week later, only to get involved in another murder case. I don't deny that he is smart, but I don't want to easily forgive him for making a joke out of me. He even pretended that I might be involved in the murder case and that I might not even be a nurse in the hospital!
[2741] Doctors sometimes like to do this. Some doctors love to joke and never care about your feelings!
[2742] I still think about Mrs. Liddon. In my opinion, she was sometimes a terrible woman, but at other times I remember how kind she was to me, how gentle her voice was, and how beautiful her golden hair was, as well as all the other things. I think we should show her more sympathy rather than blame her.
[2743] And I can't help feeling sorry for Dr. Liddon. I know he has two lives on his back, but this won't affect my opinion. He loved her so much, and it's a terrible thing to love someone like that.
[2744] For some reason, the more I see the suffering and misery of people as I get older, the more I feel sorry for everyone. Sometimes I don't know where the principles of being a good person that my aunt taught me have gone. She was a very devout person and particularly strict, and no one in our neighborhood could escape her eyes when they made a mistake...
[2745] Oh, Dr. Raeley is right. How do I end this? If I could find a powerful sentence, that would be great.
[2746] I must ask Dr. Leroy to help me think of an Arabic phrase.
[2747] Like the one Mr. Poirot used.
[2748] In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful...
[2749] Something like that.
